margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-26" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-27"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-28"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-29" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-30">m</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-31" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-32">B</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-33"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-34" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-35">V</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-36" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-37">M</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-38"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-39"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-40" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-41">M</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-42" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-43">B</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-44">S</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-45" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-46" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-47">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-48"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-49" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-50" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-51" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-52"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-53">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-54"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-55" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-56">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-57" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-58">S</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-59"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-60">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-61"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-62" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-63">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-64" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-65">τ</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-66" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-67" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-68">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>D</mi></mrow><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>m</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi>V</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>M</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><mi>S</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>τ</mi></mrow></msub></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div>where τ is the time interval for the galvanostatic current applied. <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> are the molecular weight and the active material mass, respectively. <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> represents the molar volume, and S is the surface area of the electrode. Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> are the steady-state voltage and the total change of the cell voltage <i>E</i> during the current pulse, respectively.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The pristine and LiF-incorporated samples were synthesized using a facile sol–gel process, followed by high-temperature calcination, as described in the Synthesis of NMC and LiF-Incorporated NMC Cathode Materials section. The successful incorporation of lithium was confirmed by the ICP-OES analysis, and the presence of fluorine was determined through energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). The exact ratio of the metal components in the obtained products is provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. For ease of reference, the samples Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Li<sub>0.07</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>F<sub><i>x</i>,</sub> and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Li<sub>0.13</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2–<i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>y</i></sub> will be, respectively, marked as NMC, NMC-L1, and NMC-L2. The pristine and LiF-incorporated NMC samples were subjected to powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) for phase analysis. The resulting XRD patterns and the corresponding Rietveld analysis are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. The refined XRD patterns indicate the formation of a P3-type cathode (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.826 Å, <i>c</i> = 16.876 Å, space group <i>R</i>3<i>m</i>) for the NMC material. Upon Li integration, the diffraction patterns remain the same, except for the additional peak at 2θ of 18.5° which is designated to the (003) plane of the O3 phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The Rietveld-refined lattice parameters are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> for all the samples. The results indicate that the obtained materials are biphasic in nature. The calculated P3/O3 mass percentages from the Rietveld analysis are ∼95/5 and ∼91/9 in NMC-L1 and NMC-L2, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> The mixed P/O cathodes displayed lattice parameters <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.830 Å, <i>c</i> = 16.786 for the P3 component and <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.855, <i>c</i> = 14.2336 for O3 for NMC-L1 and <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.833, <i>c</i> = 16.718 for the P3 component and <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.851, <i>c</i> = 14.176 for O3 for NMC-L2. Li ions can either occupy the transition-metal sites or sodium-ion sites. According to the obtained Rietveld-refined results and previous literature, we believe that the majority of Li ions occupy the alkali sites, yielding the O3 phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> Recently, Zheng et al. reported enhancement in the O3 phase with Li incorporation into a series of biphasic P2/O3 cathodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> However, upon Li integration, the percentage of the O3 phase increased along with a slight positive shift in the (003) plane, indicating a shrinkage in the lattice size owing to the difference in the size of Li and Na ions (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). A recent report by Stansby et al. suggests that the O3 phase is stabilized by a high alkali site occupancy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Moreover, the slightly increased O3 percentage with Li incorporation corroborates the presence of Li in the alkali site. Compared to NMC, NMC-L1 and NMC-L2 samples exhibit a reduced lattice parameter ‘<i>c</i>’ and unit cell volume, further indicating the shrinkage of the unit cell upon LiF addition. This confirms the successful incorporation of Li and F ions into the sodium and oxygen sites of NMC, respectively. The lattice parameters and more detailed analyses are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> of the Supporting Information.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/medium/am3c03386_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (A) PXRD patterns and (B–D) Rietveld refinement analyses of NMC and NMC-L1 and L2 samples, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03386&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The shape and size of the cathode materials were analyzed using TEM and FESEM techniques. The NMC material exhibited agglomerated structures with a nonuniform size of 200–600 nm and hexagonal morphology, with the diagonal length ranging from ∼0.5 to 3 μm, and the images are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>. Upon an increase in the O3 phase, for both samples NMC-L1 and NMC-L2, the hexagonal morphology became more uniform with a very smooth surface, and a slightly increased diagonal length of ∼0.6–3.5 μm is noted. The observed morphological features have been reported for a few Li-integrated cathodes which result in an increased contact area of the electrode–electrolyte interface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref43" aria-label="reference 31 and 43">(31,43)</a> These features inculcate faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics and improve the overall cyclic stability and rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The calculated interlayer spacing value from the HRTEM analysis is <i>d</i> = ∼0.240 nm, which corresponds to the (100) plane of P3 in all the samples. Additionally, in the biphasic cathodes, lattice fringes with the d-spacing value of 5.45 Å, corresponding to the (003) plane of the O3 phase, were observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>I and S2A). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Moreover, the EDS mapping confirms the presence of fluorine in the system. The F element composition deduced from EDS is 0.054 and 0.087 in NMC-L1 and NMC-L2 samples, respectively. Further, the elemental mappings confirm the uniform distribution of sodium, manganese, cobalt, oxygen, and fluorine elements in the corresponding samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S2 and S3</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/medium/am3c03386_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM, TEM, and HRTEM analyses of (A–C) NMC, (D–F) NMC-L1, and (G–I) NCM-L2.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03386&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical states of the transition metals in the cathode materials were characterized using the XPS analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>). In the Na 1s spectrum shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, the peak evident at 1070 eV is from Na<sup>+</sup>. Both the pristine and NCM-L1 cathodes display similar peaks of Co 2p and Mn 2p spectra.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/medium/am3c03386_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. XPS spectra of Mn 2p and Co 2p in (A, B) NMC and (C, D) NMC-L1 cathodes, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03386&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Co 2p spectra given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>B,D display peaks at 795.1 and 780 eV corresponding to Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, confirming the presence of trivalent cobalt species. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> The Mn 2p spectrum shows two major peaks at 642 and 654 eV attributed to Mn 2P<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, which can be further deconvoluted into four major peaks. The peaks located at 641.7 and 644 eV can be ascribed to Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>, respectively, (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A,C). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The results are in accordance with the previously reported layered oxide materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref38" aria-label="reference 21 and 38">(21,38)</a> Additionally, the tetravalent-to-trivalent manganese ion ratio (Mn<sup>4+</sup>:Mn<sup>3+</sup>) is found to be 0.62 and 1.06 in the pristine and NMC-L1 samples. The high-spin Mn<sup>3+</sup> causes the Jahn–Teller effect which can lead to structural deformation, thus reducing the electrochemical performances. The Li incorporation has led to increased Mn<sup>4+</sup> content, which we believe alleviates the Jahn–Teller effect and stabilizes the structures to improve the cyclic stability and rate capability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the NMC cathodes is evaluated in a half-cell configuration. The CV curves of the NMC and NMC-L1 samples obtained in the voltage window 1.5–4.3 V at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>A,B. The CV profiles of samples displayed the Mn oxidation and reduction peaks at lower voltage regions of 2 and 1.8 V, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Further, the sharp oxidation peaks observed at 3.4, 3.7, and 4.02 V and the reduction peaks at 3.34, 3.6, and 3.98 V can be ascribed to the Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> redox couple. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The peak below the low voltage 2.0 V indicates that Mn<sup>4+</sup> is activated after the Na insertion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> In the pristine cathode, the CV curve in the low-voltage region of 2.3–1.9 V shows multiple peaks, which is absent in NMC-L1, and the observed peaks become sharper, indicating feasible sodiation and desodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The consecutive cycles after the first cycle of NMC-L1 displayed barely any change in the peak position, illustrating higher stability and excellent reversibility. The CV and GCD profiles of the NCM-L2 cathode are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. The galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the pristine and NCM-L1 cathodes for the first two cycles in the voltage window 1.5–4.3 V are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>C,D, recorded at 0.1 C (1 C = 136 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). The GCD curves mirror the features observed in the CV profiles. It can be clearly elucidated that the multiple-phase transformations are suppressed in the NMC-L1 sample during discharge as it possesses only one plateau in the lower voltage region, while the pristine sample has two plateaus. The voltage profiles display two plateaus corresponding to Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox reactions, respectively. The lower initial charge capacity given in the first cycle was from Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from the cathode, when charged to 4.3 V, solely to Co<sup>3+/4+</sup> oxidation.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/medium/am3c03386_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. CV curves at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (A, B) and GCD profiles at 0.1 C (C, D) of NMC and NMC-L1, respectively, and (E) rate studies and (F) cyclic stability of cathodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03386&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The pristine cathode demonstrates a high initial discharge capacity of 152 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> at a 0.1 C (15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) rate, with simultaneous reduction of Mn<sup>4+/3+</sup> and Co<sup>4+/3+</sup> in the region 1.5–4.3 V vs Na. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Similarly, slightly reduced capacities of 149 and 148 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> are observed in the NMC-L1 and NMC-L2 cathodes, respectively. The slight drop in specific capacity may be attributed to the incorporation of inactive Li<sup>+</sup> species. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Though there is an initial drop in the capacity value, the overall cyclic stability increased in LiF-incorporated cathodes. It is noted that the NMC-L1 cathode is the optimized cathode due to 85% capacity retention after 100 cycles with a high Coulombic efficiency of 99% at 0.2C rate (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>F), whereas capacity retention of 74 and 58% was obtained for NMC-L2 and pristine cathodes, respectively. Recently, Guo et al. demonstrated a mixed P2–O3 cathode with Li doping for improved capacity retention, cyclic stability, and high energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The multistep plateau profile in the GCD curves of cathodes is inevitable due to the usage of redox active transition metals. Though, this phenomenon leads to structural changes and capacity fade, the optimized cathode NMC-L1 possesses long cyclic stability and better rate performance, making it a better candidate for general applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The above results suggest that Li integration greatly enhances the structural stability. Although the pristine cathode possesses a slightly higher initial capacity, the noted larger capacity fade in the pristine cathode can be attributed to the structural instability and Jahn–Teller distortions associated with the Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions. Further, the rate capability analysis was performed for the cathodes with different current densities in the 1.5–4.3 V range. The specific capacity values gradually decrease with increasing scan rates due to the limitations of diffusion kinetics of the electrode materials. Further, the rate performance of the cathode was evaluated at different C rates, and the results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>E. The pristine NMC cathode reveals a specific capacitance of 152, 141, 122, 107, 91, and 59 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 (15), 0.2 (30), 0.5 (75), 1 (150), 2 (300), and 5C (750 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The NMC-L1 samples delivered 149, 129, 119, 110, 101, and 69 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, whereas the NMC- L2 displayed 148, 127, 116, 104, 92, and 58 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> capacitances at 0.1 (15), 0.2 (30), 0. 5(75), 1 (150), 2 (300), and 5C (750 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) rates. At higher specific currents (0.5–5C), the biphasic cathode NMC-L1 displays higher specific capacities. These results clearly indicate that the optimized LiF incorporation in pristine NMC cathode enhances the rate capability at higher current densities.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na<sup>+</sup> transportation kinetics of the cathodes were evaluated at an equilibrium state using the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>A,B. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 53 and 54">(53,54)</a> The cells were charged at 0.1 C rate for 10 min and then allowed to be relaxed for 30 min for attaining an equilibrium voltage. The experimental parameters and other details are given in the Supporting Information. The calculated diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) values are in the range of 10<sup>–10</sup> to 10<sup>–11</sup> for both P3(NMC) and P3/O3 (NMC-L1) cathodes.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/medium/am3c03386_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. GITT plots for (A) charge and (B) discharge. (C) Diffusion coefficients and (D) EIS.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03386&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> values are slightly higher for the NMC-L1 sample (1.35–2.13 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during charge and 2.84–1.63 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> in discharge) compared to the pristine NMC cathode (1.15–1.70 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during charge and 2.16–0.75 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> in discharge) during sodiation and desodiation processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>C). A higher <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value indicates faster sodium diffusion kinetics, leading to a better rate capability in the NMC-L1 cathode. During the discharge process, there was a reduction in the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> values in both samples, indicating that the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion can be ascribed to the Jahn–Teller effect and increased Na<sup>+</sup>–Na<sup>+</sup> electrochemical interactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> However, it can be clearly noted that the rapid dip in the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value of the pristine sample is due to sluggish kinetics and the Jahn–Teller distortions, which are alleviated in the case of LiF-integrated NMC-L1 cathode.</div><div class="NLM_p">The CV curves of the as-synthesized cathodes obtained at different scan rates from 0.2 to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> show increased peak currents (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics is further analyzed using the Randles–Sevcik equation <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i> (log (<i>i</i>) = <i>b</i>log (<i>v</i>) + log (<i>a</i>), where <i>i</i> is the current, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are empirical parameters, respectively). A <i>b</i> value closer to 1 represents a surface capacitance process, whereas a <i>b</i> value of 0.5 indicates a diffusion-controlled process. To evaluate the <i>b</i> value, the CV curves of the half-cells were recorded at varying scan rates, and logarithmic plots were constructed by plotting the scan rate versus current. The obtained averaged <i>b</i> values for different peaks are 0.85 and 0.93 for NMC and NMC-L1 samples, respectively. The obtained values are closer to 1, suggesting a higher capacitance contribution involving rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Furthermore, the mechanism can be distinguished from a surface-controlled or Faradaic intercalation from the following equations <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 56 and 57">(56,57)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-69"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-70"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-71"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-72"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-73">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-74"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-75" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-76"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-77"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-78" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-79">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-80" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-81">1</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-82">v</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-83" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-84"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-85" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-86">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-87" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-88">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-89"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-90" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-91">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-92" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-93">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-94">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-95">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-96"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-97"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-98"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-99"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-100">i</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-101" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.111em;">/</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-102"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-103" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-104">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-105" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-106">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-107">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-108">2</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-109"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-110" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-111"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-112"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-113" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-114">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-115" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-116">1</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-117"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-118" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-119">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-120" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-121">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-122">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-123">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-124" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-125"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-126" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-127">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-128" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-129">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi><mo>/</mo><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are constants, the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> component is the capacitance contribution, and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represents the diffusion-controlled mechanism. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> represents the bar diagram distinguishing the pseudocapacitance and diffusion contributions with varying scan rates. For the NMC-L1 cathode, even at the lower scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the capacitance contribution is very high, 75%. On further increasing the scan rate to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the capacitance contribution increases to 87%, indicating the faster sodium transfer kinetics. The diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the bulk is the major bottleneck for most of the conventional sodium cathodes, thereby leading to poor rate performance. In the case of the NMC-L1 cathode, the observed dominant surface capacitance enables shorter diffusion paths resulting in facilitated sodiation and desodiation. Additionally, the EIS studies performed in the frequency range between 0.1 Hz and 100 kHz illustrate the lower impedance for the NMC-L1 sample compared to Li-free P3-NMC with a lower charge-transfer resistance, indicating better Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>D). In the Nyquist plots, the radius of the semicircle can be considered as the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), and the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values are determined as 825, 380, and 620 Ohms for NMC, NMC-L1, and NMC-L2 cathodes, respectively, indicating higher conductivity in LiF-incorporated cathodes. Warburg impedance that appears as a diagonal line in the Nyquist plot represents the diffusion behavior, and from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>D, the improved Warburg impedance can be observed in the sample NMC-L1. These results suggest that LiF integration can promote the Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics in the biphasic cathode and consequently improve the electrochemical properties of the cathodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">Additionally, the excellent structural stabilization by LiF incorporation in the NMC-L1 cathode is further corroborated by the coexistence of P3/O3 phases after 100 cycles at 0.1 C using ex situ X-ray diffraction analysis, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. The LiF integration helps in improving the morphological features for better electrolyte–electrode interactions. The Jahn–Teller effect has been alleviated to some extent by Li incorporation. Besides the Jahn–Teller effect, the layered oxide cathodes suffer from capacity fading and voltage drop at high charging voltages as the lattice oxygen escapes due to the anionic redox reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> The F ion aided in stronger TM–O(F) bonds, which resulted in the structural stabilization of the cathodes. In this regard, replacement of O<sup>2–</sup> ions with F<sup>–</sup> ions is found to improve the cycle life of both Li- and Na-ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> The incorporation of more electronegative F<sup>–</sup> ions results in the formation of stronger TM–F bond which significantly reduces the electrostatic repulsion between the oxygen layers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Consequently, the gliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers can be restricted such that the layered oxide cathodes could achieve improved structural stability and cyclic performance even at high charging voltages. The cathode with 7% Li incorporation displayed excellent Na storage ability with improved cyclic stability and rate performance. However, with an increase in the Li concentration to 13% in NMC-L2, there is a reduction in capacity value, though it displayed good stability. The noted slightly declined performance relative to the NCM-L1 sample may be attributed to the increased O3 phase and the relatively higher charge-transfer resistance, as evident from the EIS studies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a></div><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Low-Temperature Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The majority of the stable P-type, O-type, or biphasic cathodes are well studied for SIBs at room temperatures, but there are few reports on low-temperature applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> Additionally, after 200 cycles of charge–discharge at 0.2 C (30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) for the cathodes at room temperature, a capacity retention of 73.18% is observed for NMC-L1, which is far superior to that of NMC (27.53%), as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8A</a>. Further, at subzero temperature, the cyclic stability of NMC-L1 was better than that of pristine cathode at 0.5 C (75 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) rate (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8B</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Hence, we studied the best-performing P3/O3 NMC-L1 cathode and evaluated its performance at a subzero temperature of −20 °C. The GCD curves of the cathode are presented in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>A, and the specific capacity values are found to be 97, 85, 63, 48, and 32 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> at C rates of 0.1 (15), 0.2 (30), 0.5 (75), 1 (150), and 2 (300 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), respectively. The capacity value at 0.1 C (15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) is ∼65% of that of the room-temperature value, and the observed capacity retention values are comparable to few layered oxide cathodes reported previously (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8C</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>). Further, the cathode possesses superior long-term cycling stability, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>A, even after 100 cycles at 0.1 C (15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), with excellent retention, of 94% of the initial capacity and nearly 99% Coulombic efficiency. Additionally, the rate studies were examined at various current rates, and the cathode delivered reversible discharge capacities, elucidating better rate capability than that at room temperature, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>B.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/medium/am3c03386_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Subzero temperature (−20 °C) performance of the optimized NMC-L1 cathode. (A) Cyclic stability with Coulombic efficiency at 0.1 C; inset shows GCD profiles at different C rates. (B) Rate studies.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03386&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Further, a full cell was constructed to check the extreme temperature performance with an optimized NMC-L1 cathode and a hard carbon anode (without presodiation for practical applications) using 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in PC as the electrolyte, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>A. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> Prior to the full cell assembly, the hard carbon Na-ion storage properties are measured at room temperature at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> current density, showing a capacity of 183 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the second discharge cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). It is noted that the full cell delivered an initial discharge capacity of 45 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (specific energy of 151.48 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), with ∼94% retention after 35 cycles at room temperature, calculated based on the cathode mass, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>B. The full cell capacity was recorded at different current rates, 0.5 C (75 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and 1 C (150 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), where discharge capacities of 30.26 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 24.02 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> are obtained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>C). Further, the NMC-L1||HC full cell was operated at a subzero temperature of −20 °C. The full cell delivered an initial capacity of 13.54 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C (15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) rate with the capacity retention of 92% after 30 cycles and excellent Coulombic efficiency of 99.5%, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>D.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/medium/am3c03386_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (A) Schematic of the full cell, (B) cyclic stability at 25 °C (inset: GCD at different cycles), (C) GCD curves at different C rates, and (D) cyclic stability at −20 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/asset/images/large/am3c03386_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03386&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Additionally, the full cell performance was recorded at 0.1 C (15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) at various temperatures ranging from – 20 to +50 °C, and excellent capacity retention was noted at different temperatures (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The initial capacity was regained during the transition from low temperature (−20 °C) to room temperature (25 °C). At a high temperature (50 °C), the capacity slightly improved with very good cycling stability, demonstrating excellent cyclic stabilities of the full cell at a wider temperature range of −20 to 50 °C.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">To summarize, a stable P3/O3 biphasic sodium-layered oxide cathode was developed through a facile LiF incorporation strategy for applications at low temperatures. The optimized cathode displayed excellent sodium storage capabilities at both room temperature and subzero temperatures. It delivered an initial capacity of 148 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C (15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and good cyclic stability with 85% retention in capacity at 0.2 C (30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) rate after 100 cycles within a potential window of 1.5–4.3 V. The incorporation of LiF improved the performance by alleviating the Jahn–Teller effect through Li, and the presence of fluorine atoms resulted in more stable TM–O(F) bonds. The cathode material displayed excellent structural stability with a well-matched crystal structure after cyclic stability. Majority of the layered oxide cathodes or their composites are reported for SIB applications at room temperature but seldom studied at low temperatures. Even at low temperatures, the capacity retention was 94% of the initial capacity. Further, a full cell comprising a hard carbon anode and a biphasic cathode with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte displayed excellent cyclic stabilities at a wider temperature range of −20 to 50 °C (with the energy density of 151.48 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>) due to enhanced structural stability, alleviated Jahn–Teller distortions, and rapid Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> motion at various temperatures in SIBs. This strategy to engineer multiphasic cathodes will enable the exploration of new materials to push the boarders toward ultralow-temperature SIB applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c03386?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c03386</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Electrochemical studies; cyclic stability studies; full-cell and half-cell studies; postcharacterization studies; ICP-OES data; Rietveld refinement parameters; SEM, TEM, and EDS analyses; charge storage mechanism; and comparison of electrochemical activities (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03386/suppl_file/am3c03386_si_001.pdf">am3c03386_si_001.pdf (1.05 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.3c03386" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Progress on Integrated Energy Conversion and Storage Systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo Bin; Ye Delai; Wang Lianzhou</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Over the last few decades, there has been increasing interest in the design and construction of integrated energy conversion and storage systems (IECSSs) that can simultaneously capture and store various forms of energies from nature.  A large number of IECSSs have been developed with different combination of energy conversion technologies such as solar cells, mechanical generators and thermoelectric generators and energy storage devices such as rechargeable batteries and supercapacitors.  This review summarizes the recent advancements to date of IECSSs based on different energy sources including solar, mechanical, thermal as well as multiple types of energies, with a special focus on the system configuration and working mechanism.  With the rapid development of new energy conversion and storage technologies, innovative high performance IECSSs are of high expectation to be realised for diverse practical applications in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRTGcIn2S-pU8kU0YCbOU3EfW6udTcc2eZnPm-wYAauZrntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1M%252Fgslyhuw%253D%253D&amp;md5=f61e2b75fc6e14e2f6eedbee96a1486a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/451652a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.                 10.1002/adfm.201200691</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D23%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D947-958%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DM.%2BD.%2BSlater%26author%3DD.%2BKim%26author%3DE.%2BLee%26author%3DC.%2BS.%2BJohnson%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201200691&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.3c03386&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201200691&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">947-958</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuTN4-DvjpQw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2022.137468"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alvira, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Antorán, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manyà, J. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Plant-Derived Hard Carbon as Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries: A Comprehensive Review to Guide Interdisciplinary Research</span>. <i>Chem. Eng. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>447</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">137468</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2022.137468</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D447%26publication_year%3D2022%26journal%3DChem.%2BEng.%2BJ.%26author%3DD.%2BAlvira%26author%3DD.%2BAntor%25C3%25A1n%26author%3DJ.%2BJ.%2BMany%25C3%25A0%26title%3DPlant-Derived%2BHard%2BCarbon%2Bas%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BA%2BComprehensive%2BReview%2Bto%2BGuide%2BInterdisciplinary%2BResearch%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cej.2022.137468&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.3c03386&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cej.2022.137468&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Plant-derived hard carbon as anode for sodium-ion batteries: A comprehensive review to guide interdisciplinary research</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvira, Dario; Antoran, Daniel; Manya, Joan J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">447</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">137468</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are one of the most promising candidates to replace lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in grid-scale energy storage applications.  SIBs technol. is still in an early development stage and new feasible and low-cost active materials are required.  The design of high-performance anodes and the fully understanding of the sodium storage mechanisms are the main bottleneck to overcome.  Hard carbons (HCs) are extensively studied as anode material since sodium ions can be intercalated in pseudographitic domains and reversibly adsorbed in surface edges, defects and nanopores.  This review aims at providing a comprehensive overview of the current state of knowledge of plant-derived HC anodes in SIBs, which can be helpful for researchers from different backgrounds working in the field.  Working principles of SIBs are summarized, together with a detailed description of the Na-ion storage mechanisms in hard carbon anodes proposed to date.  Finally, an exhaustive literature review on the performance of plant-derived HCs in SIBs is presented, with special focus on the synthesis pathways (including activation and/or doping treatments).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqwEr880d3IW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuTN4-DvjpQw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XhsFyhurzO&amp;md5=65675a22026100a37f8eb86859b3939d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acsomega.6b00551"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mukai, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Inoue, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kato, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shirai, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Superior Low-Temperature Power and Cycle Performances of Na-Ion Battery over Li-Ion Battery</span>. <i>ACS Omega</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">864</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">872</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Superior Low-Temperature Power and Cycle Performances of Na-Ion Battery over Li-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mukai, Kazuhiko; Inoue, Takao; Kato, Yuichi; Shirai, Soichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Omega</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The most simple and clear advantage of Na-ion batteries (NIBs) over Li-ion batteries (LIBs) is the natural abundance of Na, which allows inexpensive prodn. of NIBs for large-scale applications.  However, although strenuous research efforts have been devoted to NIBs particularly since 2010, certain other advantages of NIBs have been largely overlooked, for example, their low-temp. power and cycle performances.  Herein, we present a comparative study of spirally wound full-cells consisting of Li0.1Na0.7Co0.5Mn0.5O2 (or Li0.8Co0.5Mn0.5O2) and hard carbon and report that the power of NIB at -30 °C is ∼21% higher than that of LIB.  Moreover, the capacity retention in cycle testing at 0 °C is ∼53% for NIB but only ∼29% for LIB.  Raman spectroscopy and d. functional theory calcns. revealed that the superior performance of NIB is due to the relatively weak interaction between Na+ ions and aprotic polar solvents.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzife4Ai6VvLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuTN4-DvjpQw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXktVSisbs%253D&amp;md5=a1278935588c47b91ee2d7682cf76126</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7"><span> <i>Soda

                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">On the environmental competitiveness of sodium-ion batteries under a full life cycle perspective - a cell-chemistry specific modelling approach</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peters, Jens F.; Baumann, Manuel; Binder, Joachim R.; Weil, Marcel</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Sustainable Energy &amp; Fuels</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIB) are among the most promising type of post-lithium batteries, being promoted for environmental friendliness and the avoidance of scarce or crit. raw materials.  However, the knowledge-base in this regard is weak, and comparatively little is known about the environmental performance of different SIB types in comparison with current lithium-ion batteries (LIB) under consideration of the whole battery life cycle ('cradle-to-grave').  This work provides a complete and comprehensive update of the state of knowledge in the field of life cycle assessment of SIB.  It develops and discloses a specific tool for dimensioning and assessing SIB cells, including a cell-specific model of an advanced hydrometallurgical recycling process.  It provides the corresponding inventory data for five different types of SIB and compares their environmental impacts with those of competing LIB, taking into account the full life cycle (cradle-to-grave) and an individual cell dimensioning based on electrochem. considerations.  Recycling is found to be highly relevant for minimizing environmental impacts of the batteries, though its benefit depends strongly on the individual cell chem.  Deep recycling might not be favorable for cathodes based on abundant materials and could even increase impacts.  Esp. the assessed manganese and nickel-manganese based SIB chemistries show promising results, given that they achieve at least similar lifetimes as their LIB counterparts.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoxQg8iBxWtqLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuTN4-DvjpQw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXisVOqu7rK&amp;md5=0e8d8d5afffa9d4d0d1da609e1c2f634</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201410376"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kundu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duffort, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgoJhMt6aykuA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701785"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Progress in High-Voltage Cathode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring competitive features of stationary sodium ion batteries for electrochemical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Tiefeng; Zhang, Yaping; Jiang, Zhanguo; Zeng, Xianqing; Ji, Jiapeng; Li, Zeheng; Gao, Xuehui; Sun, Minghao; Lin, Zhan; Ling, Min; Zheng, Junchao; Liang, Chengdu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the excellent abundance and availability of sodium reserves, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) show great promise for meeting the material supply and cost demands of large-scale energy storage systems (ESSs) used for the application of renewable energy sources and smart grids.  However, the cost advantages of stationary NIBs alone are not enough to ensure their com. success.  In this review, we summarize the emerging attractive characteristics of stationary NIBs, such as high-rate capability, all-climate operation, and full-battery recyclability.  Together with inherent cost advantages, these merits have resulted in an excellent compatibility between stationary NIBs and large-scale ESSs that dictates their validity in practical applications.  Representative electrode materials are highlighted to illustrate advances in corresponding features.  The insights presented in this review can inspire further research interest into NIB design and serve as a guide for the application of NIBs in large-scale stationary energy storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr7J6sqiEzloLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgoJhMt6aykuA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXks1Slu74%253D&amp;md5=6588cdb640de417fabebc198f0128326</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201900233"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, Y.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nonaqueous Sodium-Ion Full Cells: Status, Strategies, and Prospects</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1900233</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Intercalation chemistry of graphite and alkali metal ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yuqi; Lu, Yaxiang; Adelhelm, Philipp; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Reversibly intercalating ions into host materials for electrochem. energy storage is the essence of the working principle of rocking-chair type batteries.  The most relevant example is the graphite anode for rechargeable Li-ion batteries which has been commercialized in 1991 and still represents the benchmark anode in Li-ion batteries 30 years later.  Learning from past lessons on alkali metal intercalation in graphite, recent breakthroughs in sodium and potassium intercalation in graphite have been demonstrated for Na-ion batteries and K-ion batteries.  Interestingly, some significant differences proved to exist for the intercalation of Na+ and K+ into graphite compared with the Li+ case.  This review summarizes significant advances from both exptl. and theor. calcns. with a focus on comparing the intercalation of three alkali metal ions (Li+, Na+, K+) into graphite and aims to clarify the intimate host-guest relationships and the underlying mechanisms.  New approaches developed to achieve favorable intercalation coupled with the challenges in this field are also discussed.  We also extrapolate alkali metal ion intercalation in graphite to mono-/multi-valent ions in layered electrode materials, which will deepen the understanding of intercalation chem. and provide guidance to explore new guests and hosts.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq6MFV28Yn2VrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgoJhMt6aykuA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhtlGgu7nO&amp;md5=7be03349c72a71fd63a908e09c05cd85</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M.     </div><div class="casAbstract">A review with 17 refs. on the structure types and ionic cond. of layered oxides.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrMSWX_5qmIkLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgoJhMt6aykuA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL3cXhtlyksL0%253D&amp;md5=7f62855ca248a0c6a7dc63265cf948d7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701912"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Phase Transition, Air Stability, and Performance</span>. <i>Adv.
